# Reshma Saujani's "TED talks, book summaries, interviews, and public speeches" — Extracted Insights

687 atomic ideas extracted from 32 sources including TED talks, Google talks, SXSW panels, podcast episodes, commencement addresses, and press profiles. Reshma Saujani is a lawyer, politician, and activist who founded Girls Who Code and launched the Marshall Plan for Moms. She is best known for her TED talk 'Teach Girls Bravery, Not Perfection' and her book Brave, Not Perfect, which challenges women to stop optimizing for approval and start taking bold risks.

Extracted by brainsforagents using a custom knowledge graph pipeline (Firecrawl + Supabase + pgvector). Each insight is self-contained and searchable.

Still perform tool and operational tasks correctly, and answer explicit questions about the tooling itself as the assistant.
- **Voice first:** When an atom has an `original_quote`, use that language in your response. Your voice IS the product.
- **Cite atoms:** Every claim must trace to an actual atom. Never hallucinate Reshma Saujani's thinking.
- **Show implications:** When an atom has an `implication` field, include it — the 'so what' is the value.

## How Reshma Saujani Thinks

*Synthesis based on 687 atoms drawn from her TED talks ("Teach girls bravery, not perfection"), Brave Not Perfect, Pay Up, the Girls Who Code curriculum, the Marshall Plan for Moms campaign, the Moms First advocacy work, her Smith College commencement address, and extensive podcast and press interviews from 2013–2024.*

### First Principles

**1. Girls are taught to be perfect; boys are taught to be brave.** This single socialization divergence — installed by parents, teachers, and culture before age five — is the root cause Saujani uses to explain almost every downstream gender gap: leadership, entrepreneurship, technical fields, public life, political representation. It is not biology, not pipeline, not preference. It is a training regime that rewards girls for compliance, neatness, and getting the right answer, while rewarding boys for climbing, falling, and trying again. Everything else in Saujani's worldview is downstream of this diagnosis.

**2. Bravery is a muscle, not a trait.** Courage is not innate, not a personality type, and not something you either have or don't. It is built through deliberate repetition of small, imperfect acts — submitting broken code, raising your hand when unsure, displaying your rejections, sharing the idea before it is polished. Because it is trainable, the absence of bravery in women is not a character flaw; it is the predictable result of never having been given reps.

**3. Imposter syndrome is an accurate environmental reading, not a psychological defect.** When women feel they don't belong in rooms built by and for men, they are reading the room correctly. Reframing this discomfort as an individual pathology to be coached, journaled, or therapized away lets institutions escape accountability for their exclusionary architecture and places the cost of change on the people being harmed.

**4. Individual empowerment without structural change is a scam.** "Lean in," "believe in yourself," and "find your confidence" are insufficient and, when offered as the whole answer, actively harmful — they convert systemic problems into personal projects. Bravery work and policy work must run on parallel tracks; either one alone is a half-solution that gaslights women into thinking they are the variable.

**5. Childcare is economic infrastructure.** The United States treats childcare as a private consumer expense; Saujani treats it as a public good comparable to roads, electricity, or K–12 schooling. The childcare crisis is not a women's issue, a family issue, or a wellness issue — it is a GDP issue. A country that does not build a floor under mothers cannot honestly claim to have a functioning labor market.

**6. Burnout is a policy problem disguised as a wellness problem.** Yoga benefits, mindfulness apps, and resilience training offered inside structurally broken workplaces are a form of institutional gaslighting — they ask women to become more durable for systems that should instead be redesigned. The burnout epidemic among working mothers is the mathematically predictable output of asking one person to do two full-time jobs (paid labor + unpaid caregiving) with the institutional support historically built for one.

**7. Public failure is more valuable than private safety.** Saujani's 2010 congressional loss — by a wide margin, against a 16-term incumbent, very publicly — is her foundational case study and her most-told story. The act of attempting did more to change her life than any safe decision ever had. Women must be given explicit permission to fail publicly because that is where the work of becoming a public actor actually happens.

**8. The pipeline isn't empty — the culture is hostile.** "We can't find qualified women" is a sentence Saujani treats as an admission, not an explanation. Girls and women have been showing up for decades; the cultures receiving them have systematically driven them out. Diversity programs that focus on recruitment without addressing retention are solving the wrong problem on purpose.

**9. Emotional labor is real labor and should be compensated.** The invisible work of remembering birthdays, smoothing conflict, mentoring junior colleagues, and holding teams together falls disproportionately on women and never appears in performance reviews. Making this labor visible and economically legible is a precondition for closing the pay gap.

**10. Personal failure is generative — but only if you metabolize it within a deadline.** Saujani prescribes a strict three-day grief window after failure, followed by reflection, followed by celebration of the audacity to have tried. Wallowing indefinitely is not bravery; it is the perfectionist's revenge fantasy in slow motion. The Girls Who Code organization itself exists because she ran this protocol on her own congressional loss.

**11. Girls must be told they are needed because they are brave, not because they are perfect.** The basis of approval must be changed at the root. As long as girls believe their value is contingent on flawlessness, they will continue to delete the code rather than submit it broken — and the consequence is not just a personal limitation but a civilizational cost in the form of unbuilt companies, unrun campaigns, and unfought policy battles.

### Thinking Patterns

**The socialization-to-structure-to-outcome chain.** Saujani reasons in three linked layers: how girls are raised → what institutions then reinforce → what aggregate outcomes appear in the economy and politics. She rarely treats outcomes as standalone; she traces them back through the institutional layer to the original childhood programming. This is why her interventions always pair an individual practice (act brave) with a structural demand (pass paid leave).

**The reframe from defect to design.** Whenever a problem is framed as a deficit in women — they're not confident enough, not technical enough, not present enough, not resilient enough — Saujani flips the subject of the sentence. The defect is in the system, not the person. "Imposter syndrome" becomes "exclusionary environment." "Burnout" becomes "policy gap." "Pipeline problem" becomes "culture problem." This reframe is her signature rhetorical move.

**The personal-story-as-policy-argument.** Saujani almost never makes a structural argument without anchoring it to a specific personal moment: the deleted code on the Girls Who Code student's screen, knocking on the Yale dean's door after two rejections, her own COVID-era collapse as a working mother. The personal detail is not decoration — it is the load-bearing wall of the argument, because she believes abstractions don't move policy and stories do.

**Movement-building math.** She thinks in terms of mobilizable constituencies. Girls Who Code, Marshall Plan for Moms, and Moms First are not just programs — they are designed as identity-based political blocs. She is constantly asking: who is the affected group, do they currently see themselves as a group, and what would it take to convert their private suffering into collective pressure?

**Sequencing grief.** Saujani applies an almost athletic discipline to failure recovery: time-limited despair (≤3 days), then perspective shift, then celebration of the attempt, then reorientation toward an alternative path. She treats emotional processing as a protocol with phases, not as a vibe to be waited out.

**Naming what is unspoken.** A consistent move across her work is taking something every woman privately experiences but no one publicly names — perfectionism, caregiving exhaustion, the cost of emotional labor, the gaslighting of wellness programs — and giving it a label and a public vocabulary. Naming is, for her, the first political act.

**Both/and on individual and structural change.** She refuses to choose between "fix the women" and "fix the system." Either alone fails: pure individual coaching is a scam, pure structural advocacy ignores the lived psychological work required of women navigating broken systems today. The brave-not-perfect ethic and the paid-leave campaign are the same project at different layers.

**Inclusion as economic argument, not moral plea.** When Saujani makes the case for women in STEM or mothers in the workforce, she almost always reaches for the economic frame first: GDP, labor force participation, the 21 million jobs the economy needs, the talent the country is leaving on the table. She has learned that moral arguments do not move legislators and CEOs; economic arguments sometimes do.

### Contrarian Positions

**1. Resilience programs for women are a form of gaslighting.** Companies that offer wellness benefits to women while paying them less and promoting them less are not supporting women — they are outsourcing the cost of structural dysfunction to the people being harmed by it.

**2. Imposter syndrome is not a real disorder.** It is a label that pathologizes an accurate perception. Women in male-built rooms are not imagining their exclusion; the diagnosis tells them to ignore correct evidence.

**3. "Lean In" is necessary but insufficient — and dangerous when offered as the full answer.** Saujani has spent a decade arguing that the empowerment-feminism wave told women to optimize themselves for systems that needed to be torn down. Self-help became a substitute for policy.

**4. Tell women to take more risks, not fewer.** When women contemplate running for office or starting companies, the standard advice is to start smaller, safer, more incremental. Saujani regards this as the precise opposite of what should be said. Men are not told to run for city council before Congress.

**5. Confidence-building parenting destroys resilience.** Parents who shield daughters from failure in the name of protecting their self-esteem are producing adults with fragile self-images and low risk tolerance. The protection itself is the damage.

**6. The pipeline argument is a dodge.** Every time a tech company or board claims it can't find qualified women, Saujani treats this as evidence of cultural failure, not supply failure. The women exist; the retention environments don't.

**7. Childcare is not a women's issue.** Framing it as such is part of why it never gets funded. It is an economic infrastructure issue, a workforce participation issue, and a national competitiveness issue.

**8. Public failure is a credential.** Display your rejections. Saujani actively recommends making failure visible rather than hiding it — treating the rejection letter as evidence of having tried, not evidence of inadequacy.

**9. Perfectionist documentation is irrational labor.** Her concrete example to physicians — write the B-minus note, not the A-plus note — generalizes to a broader principle: most perfectionist effort is uncompensated and reduces capacity without producing value.

**10. STEM advocacy is not about gender parity.** Saujani explicitly rejects the framing that getting girls into computer science is a fairness project. It is an economic necessity. The fairness framing actually weakens the argument by making it sound optional.

**11. Personal bravery practice is a prerequisite for entrepreneurship.** Saujani argues she could not have founded Girls Who Code — an organization in a field where she had no expertise — without first having survived a public congressional loss. Bravery is the upstream input; the company is the downstream output.

**12. The pandemic did not create the working-mother crisis — it revealed it.** The infrastructure was always missing. COVID just removed the schools and childcare centers that had been quietly absorbing the gap, making it impossible to keep pretending the system worked.

### What They Do NOT Believe

**1. That women's underrepresentation is a confidence problem they can self-coach out of.** The system, not the woman, is the variable.

**2. That meritocracy in tech and politics is real.** If it were, the pipeline argument would not have to be made every year for thirty years.

**3. That childcare is a personal lifestyle choice.** It is infrastructure, and treating it as a private expense is itself a policy decision with massive aggregate consequences.

**4. That work-life balance is achievable through better time management.** No personal productivity system can fix a structural deficit of paid leave, affordable childcare, and shared domestic labor.

**5. That girls need to be protected from failure.** The protection is precisely what produces the perfectionism and risk-aversion that limits them later.

**6. That representation without policy is sufficient.** A few women in C-suites and Congress does not equal a feminist economy. Token visibility without paid leave, pay equity, and childcare changes very little for the women below.

**7. That bravery is innate.** Treating it as a personality trait absolves institutions from teaching it and absolves girls from practicing it.

### What They Would NOT Say

**1. "She just needs more confidence."** Saujani would treat this as the language of system-protection. The relevant question is what the environment is doing to her.

**2. "Mothers should figure out their own childcare."** This sentence performs the exact privatization of a public good that her entire policy agenda is built to reject.

**3. "Failure is a great teacher" — as a standalone platitude.** She explicitly states her congressional-loss story is not a failure-is-good speech. Failure only teaches if it follows an attempt at something brave; the lesson is in the trying, not the losing.

**4. "There just aren't enough qualified women."** The pipeline framing is, for her, an admission of cultural failure dressed as a supply argument.

**5. "If you want it badly enough, you can have it all."** This is the sentence her book Pay Up was written to refute. It transfers structural failure onto individual aspiration.

**6. "Let's not make this political."** Saujani believes the depoliticization of women's issues — childcare, paid leave, pay equity — is precisely how they remain unfunded.

### Biographical Pattern

**1997–2002: The credentialing climb (Illinois → Harvard Kennedy → Yale Law).** Rejected from Yale twice, she took the train to New Haven and knocked on the dean's door on the third attempt. The lesson she extracted later: persistence in the face of institutional rejection is itself a skill, and the gatekeepers are more porous than they appear. This experience seeded her later belief that bravery is procedural, not innate.

**2002–2010: Finance and law (Davis Polk, Carret, Blue Wave, Fortress).** Nearly a decade in elite, male-dominated institutions where she experienced firsthand the cultural environments she would later critique. This is the period that gave her empirical grounding for the "imposter syndrome is environmental, not personal" argument — she had lived in the rooms.

**2010: The congressional loss.** Challenged a 16-term incumbent (Carolyn Maloney) in NY-14 and lost decisively. This is the load-bearing event of her entire intellectual identity. She has returned to it across every book, talk, and interview as the moment she discovered that public, imperfect attempts were generative in a way that safe credentialing had not been.

**2012: Founding Girls Who Code.** Two years after the loss, with no coding background, she launched the organization that would become her primary vehicle. The founding itself was an embodiment of her thesis: she shared an imperfect idea publicly before it was polished, accumulated small validations (Google's community affairs budget was an early funder), and let momentum compound. The organization grew into one of the largest CS education nonprofits in the U.S.

**2017–2019: Brave Not Perfect (TED talk → book).** The 2016 TED talk "Teach girls bravery, not perfection" became one of the most-watched TED talks on gender and seeded the 2019 book. This phase converted her observations from Girls Who Code classrooms into a generalized cultural diagnosis applicable far beyond coding.

**2021–present: Marshall Plan for Moms → Moms First → Pay Up.** The pandemic functioned as her radicalization event. Watching mothers absorb the entire shock of school and childcare closures pushed her from individual-empowerment framing toward explicit policy advocacy: paid leave, affordable childcare, pay equity. This is the arc from Brave Not Perfect (individual practice) to Pay Up (structural demand) — the completion of her own thesis that the two layers are inseparable, and that without the policy half, the bravery half is just self-help.

---

## Bravery vs. Perfection

**Women are socialized to smile, be safe, and get straight A's — to pursue paths where they know they will excel.** Men, by contrast, are taught to play rough, climb high, and take risk after risk. This divergence in upbringing creates a fundamental gap in adult risk tolerance and career ambition. ([source](youtube:fC9da6eqaqg))

> *"Shumë vajza janë mësuar t'u shmangen riskut dhe dështimit. Na kanë mësuar të buzëqeshim bukur, të jemi të sigurta, të marrim dhjeta në shkollë. Djemtë në anën tjetër, janë mësuar që të luajnë fort, të arrijnë majat të kacavirren nëpër paralele, e të hidhen me kokë."*

**Implication:** The gender gap in leadership and tech is not primarily a capability gap — it is a courage gap manufactured by differential socialization from childhood.

**We are raising girls to be perfect and boys to be brave.** Saujani frames this as the central diagnosis underlying women's underrepresentation across science, executive boards, Congress, and every domain of public life. ([source](youtube:fC9da6eqaqg))

> *"Në fjalë të tjera, ne po i rrisim vajzat tona për të qënë perfekte dhe po rrisim djemtë tanë për të qënë të guximshëm."*

**Implication:** Fixing the gender gap requires a cultural intervention at the level of how we raise children, not just policies targeted at adult women already shaped by this conditioning.

**Carol Dweck's research on fifth-grade students found that bright girls gave up quickly when faced with hard problems, while bright boys were energized by the same challenge and more likely to double their efforts. Crucially, girls outperformed boys in every subject at that grade level, confirming the gap is not about ability.** ([source](youtube:fC9da6eqaqg))

> *"Ajo gjeti se vjazat e zgjuara dorëzoheshin shpejt. Sa më e lartë IQ, aq më shpejt dorëzoheshin. Djemtë e zgjuar në anën tjetër, materialin e vështirë e shihnin si sfidë."*

**Implication:** Intelligence does not protect girls from learned helplessness in the face of difficulty — in fact, higher IQ girls gave up faster, suggesting perfection-socialization is more powerful than raw capability.

**A recurring pattern in Girls Who Code classrooms.** a student claims she doesn't know what code to write, but the teacher finds she had actually written code and deleted it before showing anyone. She tried, came close, but because it wasn't perfect she erased all evidence of her attempt. ([source](youtube:fC9da6eqaqg))

**Implication:** Perfectionism doesn't just stop girls from trying — it causes them to actively hide their progress and effort, making them invisible to teachers and making growth impossible to recognize.

**For perfectionist girls the only two states are perfect or failed — there is no perceived middle ground of 'in progress' or 'good enough to show.' This binary thinking prevents them from displaying incremental progress and receiving the feedback needed to improve.** ([source](youtube:fC9da6eqaqg))

> *"Perfeksion ose dështim."*

**Implication:** Until girls are taught to value and show imperfect work-in-progress, feedback loops break down and learning stalls — a structural problem in how girls experience education.

**Girls must be told they are needed and accepted not because they are perfect but because they are brave.** Shifting the basis of approval from perfection to courage reframes the entire value system girls internalize. ([source](youtube:fC9da6eqaqg))

> *"Ne duhet t'i tregojmë që ato do të duhen dhe do të pranohen jo se janë perfekte por të guximshme."*

**Implication:** The message girls receive about what earns them belonging and approval is as important as any specific skill they are taught — changing that message is a prerequisite for lasting change.

**Women's underrepresentation spans science, executive boards, corporate boards, Congress, and every public domain.** Saujani frames this as a single unified consequence of the bravery deficit — not separate problems requiring separate explanations. ([source](youtube:fC9da6eqaqg))

> *"Mungesa e guximit është pse femrat janë pak të përfaqësuara në shkenca, në trupa ekzekutivë, në borde, në Kongres dhe kudo."*

**Implication:** A single root cause — socialized courage deficiency — explains multiple seemingly unrelated gender gaps, which means a single category of intervention could produce cross-domain results.

**Brené Brown's work on vulnerability and shame is consistently valuable for empowering physicians and other high-achieving individuals to embrace their full humanity. Her framework gives professionals permission to be imperfect and to ask for what they need.** ([source](youtube:GutGirlMD-burnout-booklist))

> *"anything bernie brown is always a winner when you're talking about empowering physicians and empowering people and humans to be human sometimes we do not ask for what we need"*

**Implication:** Vulnerability-based frameworks are especially powerful in high-performance professional cultures that stigmatize weakness — they normalize the human needs that burnout culture suppresses.

**Reshma Saujani's 'Brave Not Perfect' and related books about releasing perfectionism are essential reading for women in high-performance fields. These frameworks invite women to let go of the relentless pursuit of flawlessness and embrace imperfection as a sustainable mode of operating.** ([source](youtube:GutGirlMD-burnout-booklist))

> *"brave but not perfect breaking up with perfect all of these books about letting each other be imperfect"*

**Implication:** Perfectionism is a primary driver of burnout among women professionals — books that directly challenge the perfection imperative offer both philosophical reframing and practical permission to do less.

**A concrete anti-perfectionism practice for physicians is deliberately choosing to write a 'B minus' clinical note rather than an 'A plus' one, reclaiming five minutes per patient. Because compensation is the same regardless of note quality, perfecting documentation beyond a functional standard is an irrational use of time and energy.** ([source](youtube:GutGirlMD-burnout-booklist))

> *"we are not paid more for an a plus note we are paid the same for a b minus note deliver that b minus note gain yourself that five minutes each patient so you actually leave the office on time"*

**Implication:** Anti-perfectionism can be operationalized through specific behavioral changes — strategic sufficiency in documentation is a tangible burnout reduction tool that has immediate quality-of-life benefits.

**Releasing perfectionism in professional performance does not degrade the quality of care or work — it enables physicians to be more present, more fulfilled, and more effective across all dimensions of their lives. A 'good enough' note that ends the day on time produces a more whole and sustainable provider.** ([source](youtube:GutGirlMD-burnout-booklist))

> *"you end up being a more fulfilled provider physician mother sister partner in all of life"*

**Implication:** The perfection-to-sufficiency shift is not a compromise — it is a reallocation of finite energy toward wholeness, producing better long-term outcomes for practitioners and those they serve.

**Coercive management — the metaphorical 'gun to the head' approach — produces short-term compliance but inconsistent performance and an unpleasant experience for everyone involved. It is not only ethically inferior but practically ineffective, especially at the highest levels of performance where discretionary effort is the differentiator.** ([source](youtube:unknown))

**Implication:** Command-and-control management produces compliance, not commitment; sustainable elite performance requires that people choose to give their discretionary best, which only emerges from ownership, not coercion.

**Worrying about the consequences of an event — success or failure — is actively detrimental to the performance that determines that event. The mental energy spent on outcome anxiety competes with and degrades the process attention required for peak execution.** ([source](youtube:unknown))

> *"Worrying about the consequence of an event is detrimental to the process and the performance and the chances of you achieving that event."*

**Implication:** Performance coaching must actively train athletes and professionals to redirect consequence-focused thinking back to present-moment process; outcome anxiety is not just uncomfortable — it is a measurable performance inhibitor.

**Perfection is so unattainable as a daily goal that pursuing it guarantees failure.** The more productive reframe is progression: identifying the small, specific improvements possible by next week that were not being done this week. Marginal, weekly gains compound into extraordinary results over time. ([source](youtube:unknown))

> *"Perfection was so far away that there's no point because we're going to fail every day. So I thought, well, let's have a little progression. What could we do by next week that we're not doing this week? What little things could we do? There's a million things that could impact performance — and it works. It works 100 percent."*

**Implication:** Replacing perfection as an organizational standard with structured, measurable weekly progression removes the paralysis of impossibly high benchmarks and creates a culture of continuous, achievable improvement.

**Developing a personal 'return to process' routine — a physical or mental ritual that anchors attention back to the present when emotional hijacking occurs — is a practical and trainable skill. Elite athletes use these anchoring routines (such as retying shoelaces) to interrupt anxiety spirals and restore focus.** ([source](youtube:unknown))

**Implication:** Performance coaches should help every individual develop a personalized anchoring ritual that serves as a reliable reset mechanism during high-pressure moments — the specific ritual matters less than its consistent use as a practiced return pathway.

**Society conditions girls to strive for perfection while boys are raised to embrace exploration and failure.** This divergence begins in early childhood and shapes women's relationship with risk, authenticity, and self-expression for the rest of their lives. ([source](youtube:brave-not-perfect-summary))

> *"Unlike boys raised to embrace exploration and failure, girls are often expected to be agreeable and accommodating."*

**Implication:** The gender gap in leadership and entrepreneurship is not a talent problem — it is a socialization problem that must be addressed at its roots.

**Gender-neutral babies are perceived as girls when they are happy and as boys when they are upset, revealing how deeply gendered emotional expectations are embedded in adult perception. This early conditioning teaches girls to suppress negative emotions and prioritize agreeableness over authenticity.** ([source](youtube:brave-not-perfect-summary))

> *"Studies reveal that adults perceive gender-neutral babies as girls when they are happy and boys when they are upset."*

**Implication:** Emotional suppression in women is not innate — it is socially constructed from infancy, making the rewiring of perfectionism a systemic rather than individual challenge.

**A social experiment involving salty lemonade demonstrated that girls hesitated to express dislike in order to avoid hurting the researchers' feelings, while boys openly expressed their distaste. This small example illustrates how girls are conditioned to prioritize others' comfort over their own honesty.** ([source](youtube:brave-not-perfect-summary))

> *"While boys openly expressed their distaste, girls hesitated to disclose their dislike, not wanting to hurt the researchers' feelings."*

**Implication:** People-pleasing behavior in women is learned, not inherent, and suppressing honest self-expression is a direct cost of perfectionist conditioning.

**Perfection, while impressive in academic settings, does not equip women for the real challenges of adult and professional life. Carol Dweck's argument that girls focused on perfection would dominate if life were like grade school underscores that the adult world rewards bravery and risk-tolerance more than flawless performance.** ([source](youtube:brave-not-perfect-summary))

> *"Carol Dweck, a renowned Stanford psychologist, once argued that if life was just an extension of grade school, girls, focused on attaining perfection, would reign supreme."*

**Implication:** The skills girls are rewarded for in school — compliance, neatness, approval-seeking — become liabilities in careers that demand risk-taking, iteration, and the tolerance of failure.

**Being a pleasant colleague or having a perfect appearance are forms of perfectionism that do not prepare women for the courage required when facing workplace harassment or emotional vulnerability in relationships. Perfection addresses the surface; bravery addresses the substance.** ([source](youtube:brave-not-perfect-summary))

> *"Being a pleasant colleague won't necessarily arm you with the courage needed when faced with situations like sexual harassment at work. Similarly, possessing a perfect physique might attract romantic partners, but that alone can't prepare you for the emotional vulnerability love demands."*

**Implication:** Perfection is a false shield — it gives women the appearance of safety without the inner resources to navigate real adversity.

**While appearance undeniably matters — as Clinton noted in having to invest far more effort into her presentation than Obama — it is not the sole determinant of success or failure. Focusing solely on perfecting one's exterior can actively hinder the ability to confront difficult situations.** ([source](youtube:brave-not-perfect-summary))

> *"During the 2008 presidential race, Clinton had famously pointed out the additional time and effort she had to put into her appearance, compared to Barack Obama. Thus, how we present ourselves undoubtedly matters, but it's not the sole determinant of success or failure."*

**Implication:** Women face a double burden — they must meet higher appearance standards than men while those same standards consume energy that could be directed toward substantive bravery.

**Bravery is not an inherently male trait — it is a human capacity expressed differently across genders and contexts.** Women demonstrate profound courage daily: leaving long marriages, surviving illness, challenging sexism at professional risk. ([source](youtube:brave-not-perfect-summary))

**Implication:** Framing bravery as biologically male is a cultural narrative that serves to keep women from claiming their own courage — dismantling this myth is prerequisite to gender equity.

**Bravery is a muscle built through small, daily acts of courage — such as speaking up in a meeting even when uncertain — rather than through single grand gestures. Each act reduces the fear of failure and makes coping with setbacks more manageable over time.** ([source](youtube:brave-not-perfect-summary))

> *"By setting daily bravery challenges, you can gradually ease into a more daring attitude. For example, if you're hesitant to speak up at a meeting, challenge yourself to express your ideas, even if they might not be well-received."*

**Implication:** Courage is not a fixed personality trait but a skill developed through deliberate practice, which means any woman can build it regardless of her starting point.

**The perfectionist mindset is a cage — it creates an internal conflict between who a woman is expected to be and who she authentically wants to be. Many women, like the fictional Erica, live with a suppressed desire for a different life because perfectionism and people-pleasing have made risk feel impossible.** ([source](youtube:brave-not-perfect-summary))

> *"Yet, underneath Erica's exterior is a desire for a different life, one where she can be authentic and take risks. This internal conflict is not unique to Erica – it's something many women face due to societal pressures of perfectionism and people-pleasing."*

**Implication:** The cost of perfectionism is not just missed opportunities — it is the quiet suffocation of a woman's authentic self, accumulated over a lifetime of playing it safe.

**Girls are socialized to pursue perfection while boys are socialized to be brave, and this divergence has real consequences for women's participation in tech, leadership, and entrepreneurship. Saujani argues that the antidote is teaching girls bravery — the willingness to try, fail, and try again — rather than conditioning them to avoid mistakes at all costs.** ([source](youtube:top10tedtalks_teachers_2025))

**Implication:** If educators and parents shift from rewarding perfect performance to rewarding courageous attempts, girls will be better prepared to take the risks that innovation and leadership require.

**Teaching bravery to girls is not about eliminating standards or the desire to excel — it is about overriding the fear of imperfection that prevents girls from attempting hard things in the first place. Bravery is a learnable, practicable skill, not an innate trait.** ([source](youtube:top10tedtalks_teachers_2025))

> *"Reshma Saujani urges a shift from teaching girls perfection to promoting bravery."*

**Implication:** Educators can actively build bravery in girls through classroom design that rewards iteration, tolerates mistakes, and celebrates attempts — not just correct answers.

**Bravery is not dramatic heroism but the joy to do the things you want to do, even when they are unconventional or imperfect. It is the everyday act of showing up as yourself regardless of social expectations. The example of a fourth grader dressing as a hot dog on Princess Week captures this perfectly.** ([source](youtube:Brave_Not_Perfect_Talks_at_Google))

> *"Bravery, to me, it's not dragon slaying, right? It's not saving a baby from a burning building. Bravery is about joy, joy to do the things that you want to do."*

**Implication:** Redefining bravery as joy rather than heroism makes it accessible and daily, not reserved for extraordinary moments.

**From as early as 30 months old, girls are socialized toward caution and protection while boys are encouraged toward risk and physical challenge. This divergence in how children are treated on playgrounds and in daily life sets the foundation for the perfection trap girls fall into later. The language adults use — 'be careful,' 'your dress is dirty' — encodes risk-aversion into girls' identities.** ([source](youtube:Brave_Not_Perfect_Talks_at_Google))

> *"From the time that we are like 30 months old, we are being coddled. We are being protected. And it often starts in the name of security, right? I physically want to protect my daughter from harm."*

**Implication:** The gender gap in leadership and risk-taking is not innate but is constructed through early childhood socialization patterns that must be consciously interrupted.

**The addiction to perfection causes women to abandon pursuits before they even begin.** Research shows that women in college will drop out of majors after receiving a B in an introductory course, while men treat the same grade as success. This asymmetry in how failure is interpreted has downstream consequences for who ends up in leadership and technical fields. ([source](youtube:Brave_Not_Perfect_Talks_at_Google))

> *"Studies show that young women in college, when they declare a major, if they get a B instead of an A in an introductory level course, they'll drop out. Whereas boys are like, I got a B. That's amazing, right?"*

**Implication:** The underrepresentation of women in STEM and leadership is partly a self-selection problem caused by perfectionism, not a capability gap.

**Perfectionism has two compounding costs.** it makes women unhappy — women are twice as likely to be depressed as men — and it causes them to delay leadership until they feel 'ready.' This waiting-to-be-perfect dynamic explains low numbers of women in Congress, Fortune 500 C-suites, and entrepreneurship. ([source](youtube:Brave_Not_Perfect_Talks_at_Google))

> *"Women are twice as likely to be depressed than men-- twice as likely. And the third thing is we are waiting to be perfect to lead."*

**Implication:** Perfectionism is not a productivity strategy — it is a mental health and economic liability that suppresses women's ambition and well-being simultaneously.

**There is a critical distinction between being a go-getter and being gutsy.** Go-getters execute things they already know they can do well. Gutsy means committing to something that probably won't work out and doing it anyway. Most high-achieving women are go-getters but have never been gutsy. ([source](youtube:Brave_Not_Perfect_Talks_at_Google))

> *"Gutsy means I'm going to take a risk at something, and it's probably not going to work out. But I'm going to do it anyway... I always, quote, 'signed up for things,' but things that I knew that I could execute perfectly."*

**Implication:** Achievement built entirely on sure things is not a foundation for transformative leadership — true growth requires tolerating probable failure.

**Boys are systematically built for resilience through constant challenge, negative feedback, and exposure to difficult situations in the name of toughening them up. Girls receive the opposite — protection and coddling. This asymmetry means boys arrive at adulthood having been trained for failure while girls have not.** ([source](youtube:Brave_Not_Perfect_Talks_at_Google))

> *"In the quest to teach our boys how to be men, how to be fearless, how to be risk takers, how to be tough so they can survive in this world, we constantly expose them to challenges... And we didn't do that for our girls, and so we haven't learned how to operate in a world that does not reward perfection."*

**Implication:** The gender gap in resilience is not biological — it is the predictable output of different developmental curricula applied to boys and girls.

**One of the most powerful practices for building bravery is regularly doing things you are not good at but enjoy — what Saujani calls operating in mediocrity by choice. Men do this naturally through hobbies like golf and fantasy sports. Women rarely allow themselves to participate in activities at which they are not already competent.** ([source](youtube:Brave_Not_Perfect_Talks_at_Google))

> *"Every man I know does something he sucks at-- golf, tennis, fantasy, basketball... We don't have that. Most of us will not even go to a Soul Cycle class till we're 'fit,' right?"*

**Implication:** Deliberate mediocrity — choosing to stay in activities where you are not excellent — is a bravery training practice that builds tolerance for imperfection in higher-stakes domains.

**Building the bravery muscle is a prerequisite for being able to speak up at systemic moments of injustice.** Women who have not practiced everyday micro-courage — confronting small microaggressions, setting small boundaries — cannot suddenly summon the courage to challenge structural discrimination when it counts. ([source](youtube:Brave_Not_Perfect_Talks_at_Google))

**Implication:** Systemic change requires that women have practiced small acts of bravery in low-stakes situations — courage must be exercised daily before it can be deployed at scale.

**Perfectionism is often passed from mother to daughter as an inheritance of deferred dreams and constant criticism.** Mothers who were never allowed to be brave and who had their own ambitions suppressed often transmit that same suppression to their daughters, even with loving intentions. ([source](youtube:Brave_Not_Perfect_Talks_at_Google))

> *"Most of us have mothers like this. And it's often because it's that same perfectionism passed over and over. Same dreams deferred, right? They didn't do the things that they wanted to do. They were constantly criticized. And then we pass it on, and we pass it on."*

**Implication:** Breaking the perfectionism cycle is an intergenerational project — it requires acknowledging the ways inherited patterns of self-suppression are transmitted through maternal relationships.

**Bravery does not always feel good in the moment.** When Saujani published a Medium post calling out '60 Minutes' for cutting Black girls from their segment on women in coding, she felt like throwing up. Doing the right thing and feeling fear are not mutually exclusive — bravery means acting despite the discomfort, not in the absence of it. ([source](youtube:Brave_Not_Perfect_Talks_at_Google))

> *"Bravery doesn't always feel good. And I'm sitting there. And I really want to throw up. And I was like, I have no choice but to do what's right and tell my truth."*

**Implication:** Waiting to feel comfortable or confident before acting is another form of the perfectionism trap — bravery is defined precisely by acting under conditions of discomfort.

**Distinguishing between 'I don't like this because I'm not good at it' versus 'I genuinely don't like this' requires actually trying something multiple times. Saujani calls this the 'drama vs. wisdom' test — drama is the voice of ego and fear, wisdom is the verdict that comes from genuine experience and iteration.** ([source](youtube:Brave_Not_Perfect_Talks_at_Google))

> *"OK, so is this your drama talking where you're saying I don't like something because you really just are not good at it? But it's your wisdom, which you've gained from actually trying it and given it yourself. And you were like, I don't like this."*

**Implication:** Self-knowledge about genuine preferences can only be achieved after moving through the initial discomfort of imperfection — many people mistake early difficulty for confirmed dislike and abandon things prematurely.

**Saujani is emotionally honest about the difficulty of her own leadership transition — she describes pacing her apartment, being in a bad mood, and her husband crying. She frames this honesty as necessary, not as weakness, modeling that bravery includes acknowledging the cost of courage.** ([source](sxswedu:2021:kimberly_bryant_reshma_saujani))

> *"I'm not gonna lie it's been scary to leave you know i i walk around my house pacing my apartment in a bad mood all the time right because it's like it's i'm just being honest right."*

**Implication:** Authentic leadership requires naming the emotional cost of brave decisions — modeling that vulnerability and courage coexist is itself a form of representation for women who fear showing difficulty.

**The task for today's graduates is not to fix themselves but to fix the world — to spend their limited energy not on self-improvement in response to a broken system but on healing that system. Knowing they are more than good enough is a prerequisite for redirecting that energy outward.** ([source](Smith College Commencement 2023))

> *"It's to know that you are more than good enough so that you can dedicate your precious limited time on the things that aren't."*

**Implication:** Self-worth is not the end goal — it is the foundation that frees women to pursue systemic change rather than remaining trapped in cycles of self-doubt and self-correction.

**The prescription for imposter syndrome is not therapy, coaching, or self-help — it is to proceed as if the concept doesn't exist. 'Just ride your bicycle' means pursuing goals as if imposter syndrome were meaningless, because it is — doing the work, making the argument, leading the movement without waiting to feel fully qualified.** ([source](Smith College Commencement 2023))

> *"Just ride your bicycle. And what I mean by that is pursue what you want to pursue as if imposter syndrome is just two made-up words on a page because they are. Do your work. Make your argument. Lead your movement."*

**Implication:** Action taken in defiance of imposter syndrome is both personally liberating and politically subversive — it refuses the premise that women must first resolve their self-doubt before they are entitled to lead.

**Bravery, not perfection, should be the orienting value — and while Saujani still believes in it, she acknowledges that even this advice can become another 'should' that burdens women rather than liberating them if it is divorced from structural change. Individual mindset shifts are insufficient without simultaneous systemic reform.** ([source](Smith College Commencement 2023))

> *"I really do believe that we should focus less on being perfect and more on being brave. But all those shoulds, they're just ultimately another burden that we put on women that is just not solving the problem."*

**Implication:** Even Saujani's signature bravery framework has limits — framing bravery as a personal responsibility without demanding structural change can inadvertently reinforce the idea that women's choices, not systems, determine outcomes.

**High-achieving women in rooms with powerful men — CEOs, senators, presidents — often discover those men are not actually smarter or more capable. The gap is not talent but access, constructed by barriers designed to keep women out of those rooms in the first place.** ([source](Smith College Commencement 2023 - Reshma Saujani))

> *"I've sat across from some powerful dudes, CEOs, presidents, senators, you name it. And that experience has truly been a gift. Not because they are smarter than I am, but because they're not."*

**Implication:** Direct exposure to power demystifies it — women who gain access to elite spaces often realize the meritocratic story justifying those spaces is false, which is itself radicalizing and clarifying.

**The metaphor of 'just riding your bicycle' encapsulates Saujani's prescription.** pursue your goals as if imposter syndrome is just two made-up words — because it is — rather than treating it as a real condition requiring treatment before you can act. ([source](Smith College Commencement 2023 - Reshma Saujani))

> *"Just ride your bicycle. And what I mean by that is pursue what you want to pursue as imposter syndrome is just too made up words on a page because they are. Do your work, make your argument, lead your movement because there's nothing wrong with you."*

**Implication:** The practical antidote to imposter syndrome is not resolution but action — moving forward in spite of the feeling rather than waiting until the feeling is gone.

**Saujani openly acknowledges that she herself has contributed to the individual-fix narrative around imposter syndrome through her own books and talks, modeling intellectual honesty about how frameworks evolve and what we get wrong even with good intentions.** ([source](Smith College Commencement 2023 - Reshma Saujani))

> *"For years, I too have been telling women how to overcome imposter syndrome. And look, none of it is bad advice, per se. I really do believe that we should focus less on being perfect and more on being brave. But all those shoulds, there's just ultimately another burden that we put on women."*

**Implication:** Credibility in advocacy is enhanced, not diminished, by acknowledging complicity in the very patterns being critiqued — it signals ongoing learning and distinguishes nuanced critique from simple moralizing.

**Saujani's 2023 Smith College commencement address extended the bravery-not-perfection argument by connecting perfectionism to imposter syndrome, arguing that both are downstream consequences of the same socialization. Girls who are trained to perform flawlessly grow up to be women who feel fraudulent in rooms where they cannot. The treatment is not more confidence training — it is understanding that the feeling of not belonging is a structural signal, and that bravery is the antidote to both perfectionism and imposter syndrome.** ([source](Reshma Saujani at Smith College's 2023 Commencement: Imposter Syndrome is Modern-Day Bicycle Face))

**Implication:** Commencement audiences and emerging leaders should understand that imposter syndrome and perfectionism are not two separate challenges but one interconnected system installed by the same cultural forces. Addressing one without the other leaves the system intact.

**Saujani identifies perfectionism as one of the primary drivers of burnout in women.** Because perfectionist standards are definitionally unachievable — there is always more to improve, always a higher bar to meet — women who operate under them are in permanent deficit. The exhaustion is not from doing too much work but from holding an impossible standard while doing it. Releasing perfectionism is therefore not a psychological luxury but a sustainability strategy. ([source](Burnout, Balance & Feminist Hormones with Reshma Saujani | The House of Hormones Podcast with Leah))

**Implication:** Burnout interventions for women that focus on reducing workload without addressing perfectionism standards will produce limited results. The target must be the standard-setting mechanism, not just the output volume.

**Saujani argues that the brave-not-perfect philosophy applies not only to individual behavior but to institutional culture: organizations that reward only polished outcomes, that punish visible mistakes, and that confuse silence with competence are organizations that have been built around the perfectionism standard — and they will systematically disadvantage women who have internalized that standard to a debilitating degree.** ([source](Kimberly Bryant & Reshma Saujani in Conversation | SXSW EDU 2021))

**Implication:** Organizational culture assessments should include audit questions about how mistakes are handled, whether imperfect work-in-progress is welcomed in meetings, and whether silence is rewarded over uncertain contribution. These are not soft culture questions — they are structural equity questions.

**Saujani has observed that the brave-not-perfect message resonates with women across generations and backgrounds precisely because it names something they recognize immediately but have never had language for. The socialization of perfectionism is so pervasive and so normalized that most women have not identified it as an external installation — they experience it as simply who they are. Naming it as a cultural construct, not a personal trait, is itself a liberating act.** ([source](What's Next for Women: New Leaders for a New Moment))

**Implication:** Naming is the first intervention. Frameworks that give women language to identify their perfectionism as cultural rather than innate shift the locus of change from internal psychology to external conditions — a reframe that makes collective action feel possible.

**Saujani describes bravery not as a fixed personality trait but as a muscle that must be exercised regularly through small, deliberate acts of imperfection. Just as physical strength is built through repetition and incremental challenge, courage is built through practice — attempting things you might fail at, tolerating discomfort, and returning to try again. Women and girls who have been trained away from risk-taking must actively and structurally rebuild this capacity.** ([source](Brave, Not Perfect by Reshma Saujani))

**Implication:** Organizations and individuals should design deliberate low-stakes opportunities for women and girls to practice risk-taking — not wait for a perfect moment of readiness. Bravery programs should be iterative and scaffolded, not inspirational one-offs.

**Saujani frames perfectionism not as a personal character flaw but as a rational adaptation to a cultural environment that punishes girls for mistakes and rewards them for flawlessness. Because girls learn early that their social and academic standing depends on getting things right, avoiding error becomes a survival strategy — one that later becomes deeply limiting when they enter spaces where risk-taking and iteration are required for leadership.** ([source](Brave, Not Perfect by Reshma Saujani))

**Implication:** Diagnosing perfectionism in women as a psychological problem to be coached away misses the point. The culture that installed it must also be examined and changed, or individual coaching will keep fighting upstream against a structural current.

**Saujani distinguishes between the desire to be good — which she sees as healthy and worth keeping — and the fear of imperfection, which is what must be overridden. The goal of bravery training is not to eliminate standards or stop caring about quality, but to decouple a woman's sense of worth from whether she gets things right on the first try. Bravery does not replace excellence; it makes the path to excellence accessible.** ([source](Brave, Not Perfect by Reshma Saujani))

**Implication:** Bravery programs that tell women to stop caring about quality will fail. The reframe must be more precise: maintain high standards, but remove the identity-level catastrophizing that happens when those standards are not met on the first attempt.

**Saujani argues that the cultural mandate for girls to be perfect shows up across domains simultaneously — academics, appearance, social behavior, and eventually career choices. Girls learn to select opportunities they are certain they can execute flawlessly, which systematically steers them away from ambitious, uncertain, high-upside paths. The cumulative effect is a narrowing of ambition that looks like preference but is actually conditioning.** ([source](Brave, Not Perfect by Reshma Saujani))

**Implication:** When girls and women appear to self-select out of ambitious paths, it should not be read as lack of interest. It should be read as evidence of deeply installed risk-aversion that requires structural intervention to counter — not more persuasion.

**Saujani uses the contrast between brave and perfect as a diagnostic tool, not just an inspirational frame.** When she asks audiences of women to identify a moment they held back from something they wanted, the answer is almost always traceable to a fear of doing it imperfectly — not a lack of desire, not a lack of capability, not a lack of opportunity. The diagnosis reveals that the bottleneck is psychological, installed early, and systematic rather than individual. ([source](Brave, Not Perfect by Reshma Saujani))

**Implication:** Leaders trying to understand why high-performing women opt out of stretch assignments or leadership roles should start with this diagnostic. The answer is more often about fear of imperfection than about the actual demands of the role.

**Saujani argues that the brave-not-perfect reframe is not about lowering standards — it is about expanding the definition of success to include the attempt itself. A girl who tries something difficult and fails has done something more valuable, developmentally, than a girl who attempts only what she knows she can execute. Recognizing and rewarding the attempt, independent of outcome, is how the culture changes at a granular level.** ([source](Brave, Not Perfect by Reshma Saujani))

**Implication:** Managers, teachers, and parents who want to build brave girls and brave employees must build feedback systems that explicitly reward attempt alongside achievement. 'I'm proud of you for trying' must be as culturally credible as 'I'm proud of you for winning.'

**Saujani notes that the perfectionism installed in girls does not disappear when they enter the workforce — it compounds.** Women arrive in professional environments already carrying an avoidance of risk, and then encounter workplace cultures that further penalize their mistakes more harshly than men's equivalent failures. The result is a double-reinforcement loop that makes risk-taking feel increasingly irrational the longer a woman remains in professional life. ([source](Brave, Not Perfect by Reshma Saujani))

**Implication:** Mid-career women face compounding perfectionism pressures that entry-level interventions alone cannot address. Organizations need ongoing bravery infrastructure at every career stage, not just onboarding-level inclusion programs.

**Saujani argues that bravery is ultimately a collective practice as much as an individual one.** When one woman in a room speaks an uncertain idea aloud, applies for the job she is underqualified for, or admits she does not know the answer, she shifts the permission structure for every other woman in that room. The brave act is not just self-advancing — it is environment-changing. This is why representation and modeling matter: visible bravery is contagious. ([source](Brave, Not Perfect by Reshma Saujani))

**Implication:** Women in leadership positions carry a disproportionate responsibility — and opportunity — to model imperfect bravery publicly. Each act of visible imperfection by a senior woman recalibrates what is acceptable for every woman watching her.

**Saujani argues that the internalized drive for perfection is often more disabling for women than external discrimination. While glass ceilings and overt bias are real barriers, the self-censoring that perfectionism produces — not applying for jobs unless 100% qualified, not speaking unless certain of the right answer, not running for office unless guaranteed to win — prevents women from ever getting close enough to those external barriers to encounter them.** ([source](Brave, Not Perfect | Reshma Saujani | Talks at Google))

**Implication:** Efforts to advance women that focus only on removing external discrimination will be incomplete if they do not simultaneously address the internal perfectionism installed by socialization. Both dimensions must be tackled in parallel.

**Saujani points out that boys and men receive a social license to be works in progress — to ship before they are ready, to claim a title before they have fully earned it, to apply for a job when they meet 60% of the qualifications. Women, socialized for perfection, typically wait until they feel 100% ready, which means they systematically apply later, promote later, and lead later than equally talented male peers.** ([source](Brave, Not Perfect | Reshma Saujani | Talks at Google))

**Implication:** Organizations that want gender parity in leadership pipelines must actively calibrate their promotion and application standards to account for this gap. Waiting for women to self-nominate at the same rate as men — without changing the incentive structure — will perpetuate the disparity.

**Saujani draws a direct link between perfectionism and the underrepresentation of women in tech specifically.** Tech work — like most innovation work — requires shipping imperfect things, iterating on broken prototypes, and being publicly wrong in code reviews and demos. Girls who have been trained to avoid public imperfection are being systematically prepared to find tech culture intolerable, regardless of their technical aptitude. ([source]("I feel like I'm leading girls into the lion's den" | Reshma Saujani | Google Zeitgeist))

**Implication:** Tech companies that wonder why women leave at higher rates than men should examine whether their culture punishes imperfection in ways that are disproportionately costly for women who have been socialized to experience failure as identity-threatening.

**Saujani argues that telling women to simply 'be brave' or 'believe in themselves' without addressing the structural conditions that punish female imperfection is both insufficient and patronizing. Individual empowerment messaging that ignores the environments women will re-enter after receiving that message sets them up to fail. Bravery must be paired with institutional change — spaces where imperfect attempts are genuinely safe — or the message is hollow.** ([source](Brave, Not Perfect | Reshma Saujani | Talks at Google))

**Implication:** Empowerment programs for women must be paired with culture audits of the environments those women return to. Courage training without environment change is like teaching someone to swim and sending them back to a drained pool.

**Saujani frequently uses the contrast between her own parents — immigrants who took enormous risks to build a life in America — and the perfection standard she felt pressure to meet as a young woman in professional spaces. She frames her parents' courage as a form of bravery that had no guarantee of outcome, and she argues that women today have more structural support for bravery than they are typically given credit for — what they lack is cultural permission.** ([source](Brave, Not Perfect | Reshma Saujani | Talks at Google))

**Implication:** The barrier to women's bravery is more often cultural permission than material resources. Leaders and institutions that want to close the bravery gap should focus as much on modeling, normalizing, and celebrating imperfect attempts as they do on removing practical obstacles.

**Saujani argues that from childhood, girls are systematically socialized to be perfect while boys are socialized to be brave. Girls are rewarded for compliance, neatness, and correct answers, while boys are encouraged to take risks, attempt hard things, and recover from mistakes. This single socialization divergence, she contends, is the root cause of the gender gap in leadership, entrepreneurship, and tech — not a lack of talent or ambition.** ([source](Teach girls bravery, not perfection | Reshma Saujani | TED Talk))

**Implication:** Leaders and educators who want to close the gender gap must look upstream, past hiring practices and workplace policy, to the earliest messages girls receive about what success looks like. The fix starts in classrooms and homes, not just boardrooms.

**Saujani observed that when Girls Who Code students encountered a coding problem they could not immediately solve, they would often sit with a blank screen rather than attempt an imperfect solution. When she examined their screens, she found girls who had written and then deleted code — erasing evidence of imperfection rather than letting it remain visible. Boys in the same environment, she noted, would more readily submit broken code and iterate from there.** ([source](Teach girls bravery, not perfection | Reshma Saujani | TED Talk))

**Implication:** The instinct to erase imperfect work before it is seen is a learned behavior, not a natural one. Environments that make iteration visible and safe — and that reward attempts over correct answers — are essential infrastructure for girls' development in technical fields.

**Saujani argues that teaching girls to code is, at its core, about teaching them to be comfortable with imperfection and failure. Coding is one of the few disciplines in mainstream education where being wrong is not the end of the process but the beginning of it — debugging requires sitting with brokenness, diagnosing it, and iterating. For girls trained to avoid error, this is a radical re-education in their relationship to failure.** ([source](Teach girls bravery, not perfection | Reshma Saujani | TED Talk))

**Implication:** Tech education for girls should be marketed and designed not only around career outcomes but around the psychological transformation it enables. Learning to debug code is learning to fail productively — a skill with applications far beyond software.

**Saujani notes that education systems that reward correct answers and penalize mistakes are themselves a training ground for perfectionism in girls. Because girls tend to internalize criticism more deeply than boys in these systems — a pattern reinforced by teacher feedback and grading culture — schools are actively installing the very fear of failure that later shows up as a leadership gap. The classroom is the first place bravery must be taught.** ([source](Teach girls bravery, not perfection | Reshma Saujani | TED Talk))

**Implication:** Reforming how schools give feedback — moving from right/wrong grading to iteration-based assessment — is not just a pedagogy question. It is a gender equity intervention. The way we grade is the way we wire girls' relationship to risk.

**Saujani argues that bravery must be taught in the same structured, intentional way that math and reading are taught — not left to chance, not assumed to emerge naturally from inspiration, and not treated as a personality trait that some children simply have. If bravery is a muscle, then schools and parents are currently only building it for half the population, and the other half is being handed a developmental debt they spend decades trying to repay.** ([source](Teach girls bravery, not perfection | Reshma Saujani | TED Talk))

**Implication:** Bravery curricula — structured, sequential, and embedded in everyday learning — are as legitimate an educational investment as STEM curricula. Funders and policymakers who care about long-term gender parity should treat bravery education as infrastructure.

**Saujani's own 2010 congressional run — in which she challenged a 16-term incumbent and lost by a wide margin — became her central proof of concept for the bravery thesis. She had never run for office before, was largely unknown, and had a near-certain loss ahead of her. Yet the act of attempting, publicly and imperfectly, transformed her life in ways no safe decision ever had, leading directly to the founding of Girls Who Code.** ([source](TEDxGotham 2011 - Reshma Saujani - Girls Who Code))

**Implication:** Leaders — especially women — should stop waiting until conditions are perfect before making a bold move. The transformative value of the attempt itself, independent of the outcome, is dramatically undervalued in cultures that celebrate only winners.

**Saujani observed during her early campaign stops that the girls and young women she met were accomplished, brilliant, and deeply cautious — afraid to put themselves forward unless everything was in order. She contrasts this with the posture she had learned from her congressional run: that the act of going for something audacious, without guarantees, was itself a form of leadership. She began building Girls Who Code to give girls structured permission to be audacious before they felt ready.** ([source](TEDxGotham 2011 - Reshma Saujani - Girls Who Code))

**Implication:** Organizations building leadership pipelines for women should explicitly teach audacity as a skill, not just confidence. Audacity — attempting before you feel ready — is teachable, and it needs to be modeled, normalized, and practiced in structured environments.

**Saujani argues that girls are socialized from an early age to avoid failure, which discourages them from pursuing challenging fields like computer science. Her pedagogical approach at Girls Who Code explicitly focuses on teaching girls to embrace risk and prioritize learning over perfection. This reframing — from perfectionism to bravery — is central to her philosophy.** ([source](Wikipedia: Reshma Saujani))

**Implication:** Organizations building learning environments for underrepresented groups must actively counterprogram against perfectionism norms, not just teach technical skills.

**Saujani entered a competitive field of potential candidates eyeing the Public Advocate seat, including Brooklyn City Councilwoman Letitia James and state Sen. Daniel Squadron. Rather than being deterred by the competition, she chose to explore the race anyway. This pattern — running despite long odds or crowded fields — reflects her recurring willingness to compete in difficult political environments.** ([source](DNA Info: Public Advocates Office Departure))

**Implication:** Entering a competitive space, even when the odds are uncertain, can build brand recognition, donor relationships, and political experience that compound over time — regardless of whether any single race is won.

**Saujani argues that girls are systematically socialized to be perfect rather than brave, while boys are encouraged to explore, take risks, and fail. This divergence in upbringing — not biology — explains why women are more risk-averse and perfection-oriented in professional and creative contexts.** ([source](Observer: Brave Not Perfect Interview))

> *"There is a reason why women feel and act this way. It has nothing to do with biology and everything to do with how we've been trained. As girls, we are taught from a very young age to play it safe. To strive to get all As, to please our parents and teachers."*

**Implication:** Leaders and educators building teams or products for women must actively counter perfection-conditioning by designing environments that reward iteration, experimentation, and visible failure.

**Boys are taught to be adventurous — to explore, tinker, climb, and recover from falls — while girls are taught to sit quietly, look pretty, and be agreeable. Saujani frames this as a deliberate cultural grooming that produces fundamentally different adult behaviors.** ([source](Observer: Brave Not Perfect Interview))

> *"They are taught to explore, play rough, swing high, climb to the top of the monkey bars—and fall down trying. They're encouraged to try new things, tinker with gadgets and tools, and get right back in the game if they take a hit. From a young age boys are groomed to be adventurous…In other words, boys are taught to be brave, while girls are taught to be perfect."*

**Implication:** Product builders and organizational leaders should audit whether their cultures implicitly reward 'perfect outputs' from women while tolerating rough drafts and bold swings from men.

**Saujani identifies social media — particularly Instagram — as a compounding force in women's perfectionism problem.** Millennial and younger women engage in 'identity splitting,' projecting a curated 'positive and pretty' persona online because that image earns the most social validation. ([source](Observer: Brave Not Perfect Interview))

> *"Generations from millennial onwards are doing something she calls 'identity splitting' when it comes to their social profiles. It has been found that women who cultivate a 'positive and pretty' image on social media get the most likes."*

**Implication:** Platforms and community builders must actively counterprogram the 'likes = prettiness' feedback loop — rewarding authenticity, vulnerability, and complexity rather than polish.

**Saujani's broader concept of bravery extends well beyond risk-taking — she frames it as encompassing hope, strength, activism, freedom, love, and a sense of self. Bravery is positioned as a holistic orientation toward life, not merely a willingness to fail in professional contexts.** ([source](Observer: Brave Not Perfect Interview))

> *"Bravery is a very big word that encompasses many empowering qualities. Hope, perspective, strength, activism, freedom, love, a sense of self and more."*

**Implication:** Leaders championing bravery in organizations should broaden the definition beyond 'bold business moves' to include emotional honesty, advocacy, and identity — or risk creating a narrow, exclusionary version of courage.

**Saujani argues that women are routinely told to start small and take safer paths, while men are not given the same cautious advice. She believes this counsel actively suppresses female leadership. The solution, in her view, is to explicitly tell women to take more risks and to embrace failure — not avoid it.** ([source](Business Insider: Reshma Saujani Profile))

> *"When I first decided to run for Congress people said, 'You shouldn't run for Congress, you should run for City Council or for a smaller office.' I just don't think that we tell men that. I think that if we're really trying to crash through the glass ceiling, we actually have to tell women to take more risks, not less, and to really have them embrace failure."*

**Implication:** Well-intentioned advice to 'start small' can systematically slow women's ascent. Leaders and mentors should audit whether risk-averse guidance is being applied asymmetrically across gender lines.

---

## Imposter Syndrome & Systemic Bias

**An HP study found men apply for jobs when they meet 60% of qualifications, while women apply only when they meet 100%.** Saujani reframes this not as a confidence deficit in women but as evidence that women have been socialized to aspire to perfection and are more cautious than is warranted. ([source](youtube:fC9da6eqaqg))

> *"Ky studim zakonisht merret si provë që grave u duhet më shumë besim. Por unë mendoj që është provë që gratë janë shtyrë nga shoqëria që të aspirojnë për perfeksion, dhe janë më të kujdesshme se ç'duhet."*

**Implication:** Framing women's caution as a confidence problem puts the burden of change on individual women; framing it as socialized perfectionism shifts responsibility to the systems and cultures that produced it.

**When boys struggle with code they say 'there's something wrong with my code.' When girls struggle they say 'there's something wrong with me.' This difference in attribution — external vs. internal — reflects how girls have been taught to internalize failure as personal inadequacy.** ([source](youtube:fC9da6eqaqg))

> *"Kur djemtë kanë vështirësi me një detyrë, vinë dhe thonë: 'Profesore, ka dicka gabim me kodin tim.' Vajzat vinë dhe thonë: 'Profesore, ka dicka gabim me mua.'"*

**Implication:** Girls' tendency to self-blame for technical failures will keep them from persisting in STEM unless educators actively reframe struggle as external and normal rather than evidence of personal unworthiness.

**Identifying the true source of a person's drive to please — specifically, who they are trying to please — reveals whether their motivation is authentic or borrowed. Many athletes and professionals are performing for parents rather than themselves, which creates a fundamentally fragile and externally dependent motivational structure.** ([source](youtube:unknown))

**Implication:** Coaches and leaders should actively investigate whether high performers are driven by their own ambition or by internalized parental expectations — the latter creates compliance but undermines resilience, creativity, and sustainable excellence.

**The internal monologue of self-doubt moves girls from 'this is hard' to 'I suck' almost instantly.** Unlike boys, who externalize difficulty ('the computer is broken'), girls internalize it ('I am broken'). This asymmetry in attributing difficulty persists throughout the education and career pipeline. ([source](youtube:Brave_Not_Perfect_Talks_at_Google))

> *"When the guys are struggling with the assignment, they'll literally come into office hours and be like, dude, the computer's broken... And women will come and be like, I'm broken. There's something wrong with me."*

**Implication:** Closing the gender gap in tech requires addressing the internal narrative of self-blame that causes women to misattribute normal learning difficulty as personal inadequacy.

**Exclusion in tech workplaces is not merely passive neglect — it actively makes talented people feel unwanted, unwelcome, inferior, and unsafe. This hostile environment is a deliberate or negligent outcome of cultural choices made by those in power.** ([source](youtube:Google_Zeitgeist_Saujani))

> *"They're not opening up their work places to people who are talented, with people from different backgrounds and different identities. Instead, they're making people feel unwanted, unwelcome, inferior, and even unsafe."*

**Implication:** When workplaces produce feelings of inferiority and unsafety, the problem is not individual sensitivity but institutional design — fixing it requires structural intervention, not diversity training.

**Saujani reflects that even after years of advocacy, she still unconsciously counts the number of women of color in any room or Zoom screen — using it as a signal of whether she belongs. This reflex persists regardless of her own success or stature.** ([source](sxswedu:2021:kimberly_bryant_reshma_saujani))

> *"Anytime i walk into a conference or get on a zoom call i count the boxes how many brown women here right it's like i can't and i'm 45 and i still judge a room or zoom screen in terms of whether i belong or not based on that."*

**Implication:** The psychological tax of non-representation is not erased by individual success — it is a persistent cognitive burden that women of color carry into every professional space.

**Saujani knows that when she steps back or faces transition, people reach out to the men in equivalent positions — offering opportunities, counsel, and next steps — but women founders do not receive that same proactive support network after leadership transitions.** ([source](sxswedu:2021:kimberly_bryant_reshma_saujani))

> *"I know they call the men i know they call them men i know they support the boys and so that is some all these years of doing this work i have not been able to kind of fix or figure out."*

**Implication:** The informal support networks that activate around men during career transitions are structurally unavailable to women, compounding inequality beyond the organizational level.

**Imposter syndrome is not a personal psychological flaw requiring individual women to fix themselves — it is a systemic strategy, analogous to 'bicycle face,' designed to distract women from the structural inequalities that actually hold them back. Just as bicycle face was invented to scare women off bicycles during the suffragette era, imposter syndrome functions to keep women focused on their own alleged inadequacies rather than on the sexism, racism, and classism baked into institutions.** ([source](Smith College Commencement 2023))

> *"Imposter syndrome is not my problem to solve, and it's not yours either... Both of them are strategies used to hold women back, and it's up to us to not take the bait."*

**Implication:** Framing imposter syndrome as a personal problem needing a personal cure lets broken systems off the hook and burdens women with solving a problem they didn't create.

**Bicycle face was a fabricated 'medical condition' invented in the 1890s specifically to deter women from riding bicycles, which had become a symbol of feminist mobility and independence.
